(82): USA A(H3N2)v, 1st human death", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1276404", "date": "2012-08-20 14:25:38", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (74): USA, A(H3N2)V MORE STATES AFFECTED\n**************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Fri 17 Aug 2012\nSource: Source: CIDRAP (Center for Infectious Disease Research & Policy) News [edited]\nhttp://www.cidrap.umn.edu/cidrap/content/influenza/general/news/aug1712variant.html\n\n\nVariant H3N2 spreads to three more states\n-----------------------------------\nMaryland, Pennsylvania, and Wisconsin have confirmed their 1st novel H3N2 (H3N2v) cases linked to contact with pigs, especially at fairs, this summer [2012], as the national total has grown to 230 cases across 9 states, according to state reports and the US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC). Indiana reported the highest number of cases at 138, up from 120 reported the previous week. The swine-origin H3N2 virus contains the matrix gene from the 2009 H1N1 virus and so far this summer has been detected in Hawaii, Illinois, Indiana, Maryland, Michigan, Ohio, Pennsylvania, West Virginia, and Wisconsin.\n\nMaryland today [17 Aug 2012] reported its 1st 6 H3N2v infections, in 5 children and one adult from Queen Anne's county who had direct contact with pigs. None of the patients had serious infections or were hospitalised. Also, testing by the Maryland Department of Agriculture has detected a similar virus in pigs that the sick people had contact with at 4 Queen Anne's county farms. The Maryland Department of Health and Mental Hygiene (MDHMH) said in a statement that the findings in pigs are preliminary and will be confirmed by more testing. It said animal health officials will boost surveillance for the virus and monitor pigs at agricultural fairs in Maryland more closely for flulike symptoms. Fairs are under way in Montgomery and Worcester counties.\n\nPennsylvania is reporting 4 confirmed and 6 probable cases, all youth who participated in the Huntingdon County Fair that took place from 5 through 11 Aug [2012], according to a statement today [17 Aug 2012] from the Pennsylvania Department of Health (PDH). None of the patients were hospitalized, and though investigations are ongoing, so far authorities have found no evidence of person-to-person spread. Eli Avila, MD, JD, MPH, Pennsylvania's secretary for health, said the illnesses fit the typical flu profile, and he urged people to take the same precautions as for seasonal flu. \"People should use common sense and take steps to protect their health if they're visiting or exhibiting in a county fair in the coming weeks, especially if they are at high risk for illnesses,\" he added.\n\nWisconsin health officials today [17 Aug 2012] said the Wisconsin State Laboratory of Hygiene has confirmed 2 H3N2v cases, both with links to the Wisconsin State Fair, according to a statement from the Wisconsin Department of Health Services (WDHS). The Wisconsin State Fair took place from 1 through 11 Aug 2012 in West Allis, a suburb of Milwaukee. One case was in an adult from southeastern Wisconsin who worked at the fair, but did not have direct contact with pigs. The other was in an adolescent from the western part of the state who exhibited swine at the fair. Neither patient has been hospitalized and both are recovering from their illness, the WDHS said. The Wisconsin Division of Public Health is working with the state lab and local health departments to identify additional cases. Henry Anderson, MD, Wisconsin's state health officer, said in the statement, \"We encourage people to enjoy all their local fairs have to offer this summer, but take precautions to reduce the chances of getting H3N2v influenza.\" He urged older people, pregnant women, young children, and people with weakened immune systems to avoid swine barns this season.\n\nMeanwhile, Indiana reported that 3 more counties (Marion, Marshall, and Delaware) are reporting H3N2v cases, pushing the total number of affected counties to 23. In a statement yesterday [16 Aug 2012], the Indiana State Department of Health (ISDH) said it found some duplicated samples that were submitted to the state lab and it has adjusted the number of confirmed cases in some counties. Indiana health officials continue to investigate the cases but so far have found no evidence of person-to-person transmission, according to the statement, which said human infections are most likely in people who have close proximity to infected pigs, such as working with them in barns and in livestock exhibits at fairs.\n\nJoe Bresee, MD, medical epidemiologist in the CDC's Influenza Division, told CIDRAP News that most of the H3N2v cases are still occurring in kids and people who have direct exposure to pigs, such as spending all day in a barn with them at a fair. \"There have been some casual exposure cases, but not many,\" he said, adding that the CDC doesn't have enough information about all of the cases yet to assess the relative risks of different types of exposure. The CDC expects to get more system data from the H3N2v infections in the next few weeks, Bresee said, but so far, more than 90 percent of the infections are occurring in kids, the same level the CDC reported last week.\n\nThe European Centre for Disease Prevention and Control said no swine H3N2 virus that contain the former pandemic virus's matrix gene have been detected in European pigs. Elsewhere, Hong Kong's Center for Health Protection (CHP) announced today [17 Aug 2012] that variant H3N2 has been declared a notifiable disease. A spokesman for the CHP said in a statement that the move was made to prepare for the possible importation of the virus into Hong Kong and that the strengthened surveillance can help streamline prevention and control measures.\n\n[Byline: Lisa Schnirring]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nKen Cokanasiga <kenc@spc.int>\n\n[In previous posts in this thread it has been stated that there had been no onward transmission of the A(H3N2)v influenza virus in humans. Dr Irene Lai, International SOS, Australia (<irene.lai@internationalsos.com>) has drawn our attention to the statement in the recent CDC update [http://www.cdc.gov/flu/swineflu/variant.htm] that: \"Most commonly, human infections with variant viruses occur in people with exposure to infected pigs (such as children near pigs at a fair or workers in the swine industry). There have been documented cases of multiple persons becoming sick after exposure to one or more sick pigs and also cases of limited spread of variant influenza viruses from person to person. The vast majority of human infections with variant influenza viruses do not result in person-to-person spread. However, each case of human infection with a swine influenza virus should be fully investigated to be sure that such viruses are not spreading in an efficient and ongoing way in humans and to limit further exposure of humans to infected animals if infected animals are identified.\"\n\nHowever the recent human cases of A(H3N2)v influenza virus infection were contracted from healthy pigs at agricultural fairs. - Mod.CP\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/r/1hiS.]\nSee Also\nInfluenza (73): USA (PA), A(H3N2)v 20120819.1251882\nInfluenza (72): A(H3N2)v, (76): (North America), A(H3N2)v", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1255415", "date": "2012-08-20 14:25:38", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (74): USA, A(H3N2)V MORE STATES AFFECTED\n**************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Fri 17 Aug 2012\nSource: Source: CIDRAP (Center for Infectious Disease Research & Policy) News [edited]\nhttp://www.cidrap.umn.edu/cidrap/content/influenza/general/news/aug1712variant.html\n\n\nVariant H3N2 spreads to three more states\n-----------------------------------\nMaryland, Pennsylvania, and Wisconsin have confirmed their 1st novel H3N2 (H3N2v) cases linked to contact with pigs, especially at fairs, this summer [2012], as the national total has grown to 230 cases across 9 states, according to state reports and the US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC). Indiana reported the highest number of cases at 138, up from 120 reported the previous week. The swine-origin H3N2 virus contains the matrix gene from the 2009 H1N1 virus and so far this summer has been detected in Hawaii, Illinois, Indiana, Maryland, Michigan, Ohio, Pennsylvania, West Virginia, and Wisconsin.\n\nMaryland today [17 Aug 2012] reported its 1st 6 H3N2v infections, in 5 children and one adult from Queen Anne's county who had direct contact with pigs. None of the patients had serious infections or were hospitalised. Also, testing by the Maryland Department of Agriculture has detected a similar virus in pigs that the sick people had contact with at 4 Queen Anne's county farms. The Maryland Department of Health and Mental Hygiene (MDHMH) said in a statement that the findings in pigs are preliminary and will be confirmed by more testing. It said animal health officials will boost surveillance for the virus and monitor pigs at agricultural fairs in Maryland more closely for flulike symptoms. Fairs are under way in Montgomery and Worcester counties.\n\nPennsylvania is reporting 4 confirmed and 6 probable cases, all youth who participated in the Huntingdon County Fair that took place from 5 through 11 Aug [2012], according to a statement today [17 Aug 2012] from the Pennsylvania Department of Health (PDH). None of the patients were hospitalized, and though investigations are ongoing, so far authorities have found no evidence of person-to-person spread. Eli Avila, MD, JD, MPH, Pennsylvania's secretary for health, said the illnesses fit the typical flu profile, and he urged people to take the same precautions as for seasonal flu. \"People should use common sense and take steps to protect their health if they're visiting or exhibiting in a county fair in the coming weeks, especially if they are at high risk for illnesses,\" he added.\n\nWisconsin health officials today [17 Aug 2012] said the Wisconsin State Laboratory of Hygiene has confirmed 2 H3N2v cases, both with links to the Wisconsin State Fair, according to a statement from the Wisconsin Department of Health Services (WDHS). The Wisconsin State Fair took place from 1 through 11 Aug 2012 in West Allis, a suburb of Milwaukee. One case was in an adult from southeastern Wisconsin who worked at the fair, but did not have direct contact with pigs. The other was in an adolescent from the western part of the state who exhibited swine at the fair. Neither patient has been hospitalized and both are recovering from their illness, the WDHS said. The Wisconsin Division of Public Health is working with the state lab and local health departments to identify additional cases. Henry Anderson, MD, Wisconsin's state health officer, said in the statement, \"We encourage people to enjoy all their local fairs have to offer this summer, but take precautions to reduce the chances of getting H3N2v influenza.\" He urged older people, pregnant women, young children, and people with weakened immune systems to avoid swine barns this season.\n\nMeanwhile, Indiana reported that 3 more counties (Marion, Marshall, and Delaware) are reporting H3N2v cases, pushing the total number of affected counties to 23. In a statement yesterday [16 Aug 2012], the Indiana State Department of Health (ISDH) said it found some duplicated samples that were submitted to the state lab and it has adjusted the number of confirmed cases in some counties. Indiana health officials continue to investigate the cases but so far have found no evidence of person-to-person transmission, according to the statement, which said human infections are most likely in people who have close proximity to infected pigs, such as working with them in barns and in livestock exhibits at fairs.\n\nJoe Bresee, MD, medical epidemiologist in the CDC's Influenza Division, told CIDRAP News that most of the H3N2v cases are still occurring in kids and people who have direct exposure to pigs, such as spending all day in a barn with them at a fair. \"There have been some casual exposure cases, but not many,\" he said, adding that the CDC doesn't have enough information about all of the cases yet to assess the relative risks of different types of exposure. The CDC expects to get more system data from the H3N2v infections in the next few weeks, Bresee said, but so far, more than 90 percent of the infections are occurring in kids, the same level the CDC reported last week.\n\nThe European Centre for Disease Prevention and Control said no swine H3N2 virus that contain the former pandemic virus's matrix gene have been detected in European pigs. Elsewhere, Hong Kong's Center for Health Protection (CHP) announced today [17 Aug 2012] that variant H3N2 has been declared a notifiable disease. A spokesman for the CHP said in a statement that the move was made to prepare for the possible importation of the virus into Hong Kong and that the strengthened surveillance can help streamline prevention and control measures.\n\n[Byline: Lisa Schnirring]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nKen Cokanasiga <kenc@spc.int>\n\n[In previous posts in this thread it has been stated that there had been no onward transmission of the A(H3N2)v influenza virus in humans. Dr Irene Lai, International SOS, Australia (<irene.lai@internationalsos.com>) has drawn our attention to the statement in the recent CDC update [http://www.cdc.gov/flu/swineflu/variant.htm] that: \"Most commonly, human infections with variant viruses occur in people with exposure to infected pigs (such as children near pigs at a fair or workers in the swine industry). There have been documented cases of multiple persons becoming sick after exposure to one or more sick pigs and also cases of limited spread of variant influenza viruses from person to person. The vast majority of human infections with variant influenza viruses do not result in person-to-person spread. However, each case of human infection with a swine influenza virus should be fully investigated to be sure that such viruses are not spreading in an efficient and ongoing way in humans and to limit further exposure of humans to infected animals if infected animals are identified.\"\n\nHowever the recent human cases of A(H3N2)v influenza virus infection were contracted from healthy pigs at agricultural fairs. - Mod.CP\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/r/1hiS.]\nSee Also\nInfluenza (73): USA (PA), A(H3N2)v 20120819.1251882\nInfluenza (72): A(H3N2)v, asymptomatic pigs 20120819.1251848\nInfluenza (71): WHO update: 20120818.1250752\nInfluenza (70): A(H3N2)v update 20120817.1249415\nInfluenza (69): A(H3N2)v, CDC update 20120811.1239668\nInfluenza (68): A(H3N2)v, age-related seroprotection 20120810.1238403\nInfluenza (67): USA (IN, OH), A(H3N2)v, more cases 20120809.1236861\nInfluenza (66): USA (OH), A/(H3N2)v, infected pigs 20120807.1232623\nInfluenza (65): swine influenza, A/(H3N2)v, OIE status 20120806.1229963\nInfluenza (64): USA, A/(H3N2)v, CDC report 20120805.1228593\nInfluenza (63): WHO update (73): USA (PA), A(H3N2)v", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1251882", "date": "2012-08-06 10:10:45", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (65): SWINE INFLUENZA, A/(H3N2)V, OIE STATUS\n******************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Mon 6 Aug 2012\nFrom: Arnon Shimshony, ProMED-mail Animal Disease and Zoonoses Moderator <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n\nSwine influenza - OIE status\n----------------------------\nThe undersigned's commentary to ProMED-mail's posting \"Influenza (64): USA A/(H3N2)v CDC report,\" archive number 20120805.1228593, suggested that the subclinical or mild cases of influenza virus A/(H3N2)v recently recorded in swine in the USA are \"not regarded as OIE-notifiable swine influenza.\"\n\nThis is in need of correction and clarification.\n\nSwine influenza is not a listed disease with the OIE. The current list includes the following 7 swine diseases: African swine fever, classical swine fever, Nipah virus encephalitis, porcine cysticercosis, porcine reproductive and respiratory syndrome, swine vesicular disease and transmissible gastroenteritis.\n\nAs laid out in OIE's Disease Information Summary on Swine Influenza (http://tinyurl.com/brs96wx), the reason for the disease not being listed is explained by its low mortality rate and its being a \"very uncommonly documented human disease.\" Accordingly, it does not meet OIE criteria for disease notification. The criteria that are democratically adopted by all OIE Members can be found in chapter 1.2 of the OIE Terrestrial Animal Health Code; see http://www.oie.int/index.php?id=169&L=0&htmfile=chapitre_1.1.2.htm.\n\nOIE's definition of \"Emerging disease\" is: \"A new infection resulting from the evolution or change of an existing pathogenic agent, a known infection spreading to a new geographic area or population, or a previously unrecognized pathogenic agent or disease diagnosed for the 1st time and which has a significant impact on animal or public health.\"\n\nA non-listed animal disease is officially required to be immediately notified if determined as \"an emerging disease with significant morbidity or mortality, or zoonotic potential.\" This is spelled out in Article 1.1.3.1.e of the Terrestrial Animal Health Code.\n\nIn 2009/2010, countries from 3 continents notified to the OIE diagnosed cases in swine and other animal species of \"Pandemic A/H1N1 virus (2009),\" caused by the novel A(H1N1)pdm09 virus, as an \"emerging disease.\" The OIE encourages members to carry out surveillance and reporting of the presence of all epidemiologic events of significance in animals due to influenza viruses.\n\nA joint OIE-FAO network of expertise on influenza, called OFFLU, was established in 2005. It initially engaged in international efforts to monitor and control infections of avian influenza in poultry and other bird species and to share biological material and data to support early development of human pandemic vaccines, and later expanded its role to cover all animal influenza viruses, including swine influenza.\n\nA chapter on swine influenza is included in OIE's Manual of Diagnostic Tests and Vaccines for Terrestrial Animals. A new version of the said chapter 2.8.8. was adopted by OIE's General Session, Paris, May 2010 (available on-line at http://www.oie.int/fileadmin/Home/eng/Health_standards/tahm/2.08.08_SWINE_INFLUENZA.pdf).\n\n--\nArnon Shimshony\nProMED-mail Animal Disease and Zoonoses Moderator\n<promed@promedmail.org>\nSee Also\nInfluenza (64): USA, A/(H3N2)v, CDC report 20120805.1228593\n2010\n----\nInfluenza pandemic (H1N1) (64): WHO, pandemic over 20100811.2753\nInfluenza pandemic (H1N1), animal (04): global, update 20100131.0337\n.................................................arn/msp/lm", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "nipah virus", "encephalitis"], "syndromes": ["encephalitis"], "event_date": ["2065-03-16 00:00:00", "2064-03-16 00:00:00", "2009-03-16 00:00:00", "2005-03-16 00:00:00", "2010-05-16 00:00:00", "2064-03-16 00:00:00", "2010-03-16 00:00:00", "2064-03-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-04 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Swine", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "USA", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Paris", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "1251848", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Influenza (72):  A(H3N2)v, asymptomatic pigs", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1251848", "date": "2012-08-06 10:10:45", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (65): SWINE INFLUENZA, A/(H3N2)V, OIE STATUS\n******************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Mon 6 Aug 2012\nFrom: Arnon Shimshony, ProMED-mail Animal Disease and Zoonoses Moderator <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n\nSwine influenza - The criteria that are democratically adopted by all OIE Members can be found in chapter 1.2 of the OIE Terrestrial Animal Health Code; see http://www.oie.int/index.php?id=169&L=0&htmfile=chapitre_1.1.2.htm.\n\nOIE's definition of \"Emerging disease\" is: \"A new infection resulting from the evolution or change of an existing pathogenic agent, a known infection spreading to a new geographic area or population, or a previously unrecognized pathogenic agent or disease diagnosed for the 1st time and which has a significant impact on animal or public health.\"\n\nA non-listed animal disease is officially required to be immediately notified if determined as \"an emerging disease with significant morbidity or mortality, or zoonotic potential.\" This is spelled out in Article 1.1.3.1.e of the Terrestrial Animal Health Code.\n\nIn 2009/2010, countries from 3 continents notified to the OIE diagnosed cases in swine and other animal species of \"Pandemic A/H1N1 virus (2009),\" caused by the novel A(H1N1)pdm09 virus, as an \"emerging disease.\" The OIE encourages members to carry out surveillance and reporting of the presence of all epidemiologic events of significance in animals due to influenza viruses.\n\nA joint OIE-FAO network of expertise on influenza, called OFFLU, was established in 2005. It initially engaged in international efforts to monitor and control infections of avian influenza in poultry and other bird species and to share biological material and data to support early development of human pandemic vaccines, and later expanded its role to cover all animal influenza viruses, including swine influenza.\n\nA chapter on swine influenza is included in OIE's Manual of Diagnostic Tests and Vaccines for Terrestrial Animals. A new version of the said chapter 2.8.8. was adopted by OIE's General Session, Paris, May 2010 (available on-line at http://www.oie.int/fileadmin/Home/eng/Health_standards/tahm/2.08.08_SWINE_INFLUENZA.pdf).\n\n--\nArnon Shimshony\nProMED-mail Animal Disease and Zoonoses Moderator\n<promed@promedmail.org>\nSee Also\nInfluenza (64): USA, A/(H3N2)v, CDC report 20120805.1228593\n2010\n----\nInfluenza pandemic (H1N1) (64): WHO, pandemic over 20100811.2753\nInfluenza pandemic (H1N1), animal (04): global, update 20100131.0337\n.................................................arn/msp/lm", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "nipah virus", "encephalitis"], "syndromes": ["encephalitis"], "event_date": ["2065-03-16 00:00:00", "2064-03-16 00:00:00", "2009-03-16 00:00:00", "2005-03-16 00:00:00", "2010-05-16 00:00:00", "2064-03-16 00:00:00", "2010-03-16 00:00:00", "2064-03-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-04 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Swine", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "USA", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Paris", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "1249415", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Influenza (70): A(H3N2)v update", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1249415", "date": "2012-08-06 10:10:45", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (65): SWINE INFLUENZA, A/(H3N2)V, OIE STATUS\n******************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Mon 6 Aug 2012\nFrom: Arnon Shimshony, ProMED-mail Animal Disease and Zoonoses Moderator <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n\nSwine influenza - The criteria that are democratically adopted by all OIE Members can be found in chapter 1.2 of the OIE Terrestrial Animal Health Code; see http://www.oie.int/index.php?id=169&L=0&htmfile=chapitre_1.1.2.htm.\n\nOIE's definition of \"Emerging disease\" is: \"A new infection resulting from the evolution or change of an existing pathogenic agent, a known infection spreading to a new geographic area or population, or a previously unrecognized pathogenic agent or disease diagnosed for the 1st time and which has a significant impact on animal or public health.\"\n\nA non-listed animal disease is officially required to be immediately notified if determined as \"an emerging disease with significant morbidity or mortality, or zoonotic potential.\" This is spelled out in Article 1.1.3.1.e of the Terrestrial Animal Health Code.\n\nIn 2009/2010, countries from 3 continents notified to the OIE diagnosed cases in swine and other animal species of \"Pandemic A/H1N1 virus (2009),\" caused by the novel A(H1N1)pdm09 virus, as an \"emerging disease.\" The OIE encourages members to carry out surveillance and reporting of the presence of all epidemiologic events of significance in animals due to influenza viruses.\n\nA joint OIE-FAO network of expertise on influenza, called OFFLU, was established in 2005. It initially engaged in international efforts to monitor and control infections of avian influenza in poultry and other bird species and to share biological material and data to support early development of human pandemic vaccines, and later expanded its role to cover all animal influenza viruses, including swine influenza.\n\nA chapter on swine influenza is included in OIE's Manual of Diagnostic Tests and Vaccines for Terrestrial Animals. A new version of the said chapter 2.8.8. was adopted by OIE's General Session, Paris, May 2010 (available on-line at http://www.oie.int/fileadmin/Home/eng/Health_standards/tahm/2.08.08_SWINE_INFLUENZA.pdf).\n\n--\nArnon Shimshony\nProMED-mail Animal Disease and Zoonoses Moderator\n<promed@promedmail.org>\nSee Also\nInfluenza (64): USA, A/(H3N2)v, CDC report 20120805.1228593\n2010\n----\nInfluenza pandemic (H1N1) (64): WHO, pandemic over 20100811.2753\nInfluenza pandemic (H1N1), animal (04): global, update 20100131.0337\n.................................................arn/msp/lm", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "nipah virus", "encephalitis"], "syndromes": ["encephalitis"], "event_date": ["2065-03-16 00:00:00", "2064-03-16 00:00:00", "2009-03-16 00:00:00", "2005-03-16 00:00:00", "2010-05-16 00:00:00", "2064-03-16 00:00:00", "2010-03-16 00:00:00", "2064-03-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-04 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Swine", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "USA", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Paris", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "1228593", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Influenza (64): USA, A/(H3N2)v, CDC report", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1228593", "date": "2012-01-29 18:32:14", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (05): EUROPE, H3N2 PREDOMINANT\n****************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Fri 27 Jan 2012\nSource: EuroFlu - Weekly Electronic Bulletin [abreviated & edited]\nhttp://www.euroflu.org/cgi-files/bulletin_v2.cgi\n\nInfluenza activity continues to rise slowly in the WHO European Region due to A(H3N2)\n---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------\nSummary\n\nInfluenza activity continues to rise slowly in the WHO European Region due to A(H3N2)\n\u2022 This issue is based on data for week 3/2012 reported by 46 Member States in the WHO European Region. Consultation rates for influenza-like illness (ILI) and acute respiratory infection (ARI) continue to be relatively low in most countries of the Region, but with many countries reporting increasing activity.\n\u2022 29 % of sentinel specimens tested positive for influenza, which is an increase over last week: 95% of these were influenza A.\n\u2022 Of the 352 influenza A viruses from sentinel sources that were subtyped, 99 % were A(H3N2).\n\nCurrent situation: week 3/2012\n\nTrends in clinical activity were reported by 42 countries: 20 stable, 19 increasing and 3 decreasing. At the same time, ILI and ARI consultation rates remained at low levels in most countries of the WHO European Region. Similar to the previous week, consultation rates were highest in young children. Of the 41 countries reporting on the geographical spread of influenza: 12 reported no activity; 7 local activity; 18 sporadic activity; and 4 countries (Finland, Iceland, Italy and Spain) regional activity. Intensity was reported to be low in most countries reporting on this indicator, except for Bulgaria, Iceland, Italy and Spain, where influenza activity was at medium levels.\n\nSevere acute respiratory infection (SARI): week 3/2012\n\n11 countries reported data from sentinel hospital-based surveillance for SARI: Albania, Armenia, Belarus, Georgia, Kazakhstan, Kyrgyzstan, the Republic of Moldova, Romania, the Russian Federation, Serbia and Ukraine. The number of SARI hospitalizations remained largely unchanged compared to the previous week, except in Kazakhstan, which reported increases in SARI hospitalization \u2013 mainly in children aged 0\u20134 years. A total of 122 respiratory specimens was collected from SARI patients, of which 10 (8%) tested positive for influenza with 6 being subtyped, all as A(H3N2). 6 countries so far (Albania, Belarus, Kazakhstan, Kyrgyzstan, Romania and Ukraine) have reported influenza detections in SARI patients during the course of this influenza season.\n\nVirological situation: week 3/2012\n\nSentinel outpatient clinics collected 1469 respiratory specimens, 422 (29 %) of which tested positive for influenza viruses: 401 (95%) were type A and 21 (5%) were type B. Of the 352 influenza A viruses subtyped, 349 (99%) were influenza A(H3N2) and 3 (1%) were A(H1N1)pdm09. In the 18 countries testing 20 or more sentinel specimens, influenza positivity ranged from 0% to 85%, with a median of 22% (mean: 25%). In addition, 525 non-sentinel specimens were reported positive for influenza: 504 (96%) type A and 21 (4%) type B. Of the influenza A viruses, 189 were subtyped: 173 (92%) as A(H3N2) and 16 (8%) as A(H1N1)pdm09.\n\nCumulative virological data: weeks 40/2011 \u2013 3/2012\n\nDuring this period, 3436 influenza virus detections were reported: 3226 (94%) were influenza A and 210 (6%) were influenza B. Of the influenza A viruses, 2099 were subtyped: 1985 (95%) as A(H3N2) and 114 (5%) as A(H1N1)pdm09.\n\nIn addition, 56 influenza viruses were characterized antigenically: 46 were A(H3) A/Perth/16/2009 (H3N2)-like; 2 were A(H1N1)pdm09 A/California/7/2009 (H1N1)-like; 2 were B/Florida/4/2006-like (B/Yamagata/16/88 lineage); 2 were B/Bangladesh/3333/2007-like (B/Yamagata/16/88 lineage); and 4 were B/Brisbane/60/2008-like (B/Victoria/2/87 lineage). Of 95 influenza viruses characterized genetically, 1 belonged to the A(H1N1)pdm09 group represented by A/Astrakhan/1/2011; 1 belonged to the A(H1N1)pdm09 group represented by A/St Petersburg/100/2011; 5 belonged to the B/Bangladesh/3333/2007 clade (Yamagata lineage); 2 belonged to the B/Brisbane/60/2008 clade (Victoria lineage); and 86 belonged to the A/Victoria/208/2009 A(H3) clade \u2013 63 in the group represented by A/Stockholm/18/2011 (group 3), 19 in the group represented by A/Iowa/19/2010 (group 6) and 1 in the group represented by A/Johannesburg/114/2011(group 7).\n\nComment\n\nInfluenza activity has increased consistently over the past several weeks, but influenza detections remain low compared to the same period of the 2010/2011 season. Influenza A and B viruses are co-circulating in the Region, with the vast majority of detections in both outpatient clinics and hospitals being A(H3N2).\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[These data are consistent with the impression that the onset of the annual seasonal influenza season is delayed. Currently the predominant virus isolated throughout Europe is an A/(H3N2) virus and not the previously predominant A/H1N1)pdm virus. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\nInfluenza (04): Japan (SA), nosocomial Of 95 influenza viruses characterized genetically, 1 belonged to the A(H1N1)pdm09 group represented by A/Astrakhan/1/2011; 1 belonged to the A(H1N1)pdm09 group represented by A/St Petersburg/100/2011; 5 belonged to the B/Bangladesh/3333/2007 clade (Yamagata lineage); 2 belonged to the B/Brisbane/60/2008 clade (Victoria lineage); and 86 belonged to the A/Victoria/208/2009 A(H3) clade \u2013 63 in the group represented by A/Stockholm/18/2011 (group 3), 19 in the group represented by A/Iowa/19/2010 (group 6) and 1 in the group represented by A/Johannesburg/114/2011(group 7).\n\nComment\n\nInfluenza activity has increased consistently over the past several weeks, but influenza detections remain low compared to the same period of the 2010/2011 season. Influenza A and B viruses are co-circulating in the Region, with the vast majority of detections in both outpatient clinics and hospitals being A(H3N2).\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[These data are consistent with the impression that the onset of the annual seasonal influenza season is delayed. Currently the predominant virus isolated throughout Europe is an A/(H3N2) virus and not the previously predominant A/H1N1)pdm virus. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\nInfluenza (04): Japan (SA), nosocomial "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1214922", "date": "2012-01-29 18:32:14", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (05): EUROPE, H3N2 PREDOMINANT\n****************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Fri 27 Jan 2012\nSource: EuroFlu - Weekly Electronic Bulletin [abreviated & edited]\nhttp://www.euroflu.org/cgi-files/bulletin_v2.cgi\n\nInfluenza activity continues to rise slowly in the WHO European Region due to A(H3N2)\n---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------\nSummary\n\nInfluenza activity continues to rise slowly in the WHO European Region due to A(H3N2)\n\u2022 This issue is based on data for week 3/2012 reported by 46 Member States in the WHO European Region. Consultation rates for influenza-like illness (ILI) and acute respiratory infection (ARI) continue to be relatively low in most countries of the Region, but with many countries reporting increasing activity.\n\u2022 29 % of sentinel specimens tested positive for influenza, which is an increase over last week: 95% of these were influenza A.\n\u2022 Of the 352 influenza A viruses from sentinel sources that were subtyped, 99 % were A(H3N2).\n\nCurrent situation: week 3/2012\n\nTrends in clinical activity were reported by 42 countries: 20 stable, 19 increasing and 3 decreasing. At the same time, ILI and ARI consultation rates remained at low levels in most countries of the WHO European Region. Similar to the previous week, consultation rates were highest in young children. Of the 41 countries reporting on the geographical spread of influenza: 12 reported no activity; 7 local activity; 18 sporadic activity; and 4 countries (Finland, Iceland, Italy and Spain) regional activity. Intensity was reported to be low in most countries reporting on this indicator, except for Bulgaria, Iceland, Italy and Spain, where influenza activity was at medium levels.\n\nSevere acute respiratory infection (SARI): week 3/2012\n\n11 countries reported data from sentinel hospital-based surveillance for SARI: Albania, Armenia, Belarus, Georgia, Kazakhstan, Kyrgyzstan, the Republic of Moldova, Romania, the Russian Federation, Serbia and Ukraine. The number of SARI hospitalizations remained largely unchanged compared to the previous week, except in Kazakhstan, which reported increases in SARI hospitalization \u2013 mainly in children aged 0\u20134 years. A total of 122 respiratory specimens was collected from SARI patients, of which 10 (8%) tested positive for influenza with 6 being subtyped, all as A(H3N2). 6 countries so far (Albania, Belarus, Kazakhstan, Kyrgyzstan, Romania and Ukraine) have reported influenza detections in SARI patients during the course of this influenza season.\n\nVirological situation: week 3/2012\n\nSentinel outpatient clinics collected 1469 respiratory specimens, 422 (29 %) of which tested positive for influenza viruses: 401 (95%) were type A and 21 (5%) were type B. Of the 352 influenza A viruses subtyped, 349 (99%) were influenza A(H3N2) and 3 (1%) were A(H1N1)pdm09. In the 18 countries testing 20 or more sentinel specimens, influenza positivity ranged from 0% to 85%, with a median of 22% (mean: 25%). In addition, 525 non-sentinel specimens were reported positive for influenza: 504 (96%) type A and 21 (4%) type B. Of the influenza A viruses, 189 were subtyped: 173 (92%) as A(H3N2) and 16 (8%) as A(H1N1)pdm09.\n\nCumulative virological data: weeks 40/2011 \u2013 3/2012\n\nDuring this period, 3436 influenza virus detections were reported: 3226 (94%) were influenza A and 210 (6%) were influenza B. Of the influenza A viruses, 2099 were subtyped: 1985 (95%) as A(H3N2) and 114 (5%) as A(H1N1)pdm09.\n\nIn addition, 56 influenza viruses were characterized antigenically: 46 were A(H3) A/Perth/16/2009 (H3N2)-like; 2 were A(H1N1)pdm09 A/California/7/2009 (H1N1)-like; 2 were B/Florida/4/2006-like (B/Yamagata/16/88 lineage); 2 were B/Bangladesh/3333/2007-like (B/Yamagata/16/88 lineage); and 4 were B/Brisbane/60/2008-like (B/Victoria/2/87 lineage). Of 95 influenza viruses characterized genetically, 1 belonged to the A(H1N1)pdm09 group represented by A/Astrakhan/1/2011; 1 belonged to the A(H1N1)pdm09 group represented by A/St Petersburg/100/2011; 5 belonged to the B/Bangladesh/3333/2007 clade (Yamagata lineage); 2 belonged to the B/Brisbane/60/2008 clade (Victoria lineage); and 86 belonged to the A/Victoria/208/2009 A(H3) clade \u2013 63 in the group represented by A/Stockholm/18/2011 (group 3), 19 in the group represented by A/Iowa/19/2010 (group 6) and 1 in the group represented by A/Johannesburg/114/2011(group 7).\n\nComment\n\nInfluenza activity has increased consistently over the past several weeks, but influenza detections remain low compared to the same period of the 2010/2011 season. Influenza A and B viruses are co-circulating in the Region, with the vast majority of detections in both outpatient clinics and hospitals being A(H3N2).\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[These data are consistent with the impression that the onset of the annual seasonal influenza season is delayed. In the 18 countries testing 20 or more sentinel specimens, influenza positivity ranged from 0% to 85%, with a median of 22% (mean: 25%). In addition, 525 non-sentinel specimens were reported positive for influenza: 504 (96%) type A and 21 (4%) type B. Of the influenza A viruses, 189 were subtyped: 173 (92%) as A(H3N2) and 16 (8%) as A(H1N1)pdm09.\n\nCumulative virological data: weeks 40/2011 \u2013 3/2012\n\nDuring this period, 3436 influenza virus detections were reported: 3226 (94%) were influenza A and 210 (6%) were influenza B. Of the influenza A viruses, 2099 were subtyped: 1985 (95%) as A(H3N2) and 114 (5%) as A(H1N1)pdm09.\n\nIn addition, 56 influenza viruses were characterized antigenically: 46 were A(H3) A/Perth/16/2009 (H3N2)-like; 2 were A(H1N1)pdm09 A/California/7/2009 (H1N1)-like; 2 were B/Florida/4/2006-like (B/Yamagata/16/88 lineage); 2 were B/Bangladesh/3333/2007-like (B/Yamagata/16/88 lineage); and 4 were B/Brisbane/60/2008-like (B/Victoria/2/87 lineage). Of 95 influenza viruses characterized genetically, 1 belonged to the A(H1N1)pdm09 group represented by A/Astrakhan/1/2011; 1 belonged to the A(H1N1)pdm09 group represented by A/St Petersburg/100/2011; 5 belonged to the B/Bangladesh/3333/2007 clade (Yamagata lineage); 2 belonged to the B/Brisbane/60/2008 clade (Victoria lineage); and 86 belonged to the A/Victoria/208/2009 A(H3) clade \u2013 63 in the group represented by A/Stockholm/18/2011 (group 3), 19 in the group represented by A/Iowa/19/2010 (group 6) and 1 in the group represented by A/Johannesburg/114/2011(group 7).\n\nComment\n\nInfluenza activity has increased consistently over the past several weeks, but influenza detections remain low compared to the same period of the 2010/2011 season. Influenza A and B viruses are co-circulating in the Region, with the vast majority of detections in both outpatient clinics and hospitals being A(H3N2).\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[These data are consistent with the impression that the onset of the annual seasonal influenza season is delayed. Currently the predominant virus isolated throughout Europe is an A/(H3N2) virus and not the previously predominant A/H1N1)pdm virus. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\nInfluenza (04): Japan (SA), nosocomial "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=989540", "date": "2011-11-05 23:16:17", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (66): UNITED STATES OF AMERICA SWINE-ORIGIN H3N2\nREASSORTANT, UPDATE\n*******************************************************************************\n\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Fri 4 Nov 2011\nSource: The Record.com and the Canadian Press [edited]\nhttp://www.therecord.com/living/article/620441--u-s-spots-two-more-human-cases-of-flu-with-new-swine-virus-but-cases-not-linked\n\n\nMore human cases of flu with new swine virus\n--------------------------------------------\nTwo new cases of human infection with a flu virus that has been\nsporadically jumping to people from pigs have been spotted in the\nUnited States, the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC)\nreported Friday [4 Nov 2011]. The new cases, in Maine and Indiana,\nbring to 7 the number seen in the U.S. since July [2011]. To date\nCanada has seen no infections with this virus, the National\nMicrobiology Laboratory said.\n\nWhile the infections have been mainly mild, the CDC is keeping a close\neye on the situation. The head of the Atlanta-based agency's influenza\ndivision said a seed strain for a vaccine that would protect against\nthis virus has already been developed and has been given to vaccine\nmanufacturers. \"We're really trying to be in front of events in terms\nof preparedness,\" Dr. Nancy Cox said in an interview. \"We're being\nvery vigilant because we realize that there's a portion of the\npopulation ... that really has very little cross-reactive antibodies\nto the swine reassortant virus.\"\n\nThe group Cox was referring to is children. Most of the infections --\n2 from Maine, 3 from Pennsylvania and 2 from Indiana - have been in\nyoung children, though Cox noted one of the cases was a 59-year-old\nperson. Most of the people who have been infected had close contact\nwith pigs and the 2 latest cases are no exception.\n\nDr. Stephen Sears, state epidemiologist for Maine, said the 2 children\nfrom his state who were infected both had a lot of contact with pigs.\nWhile they live in the same region of Maine, the children did not have\ncontact with each other. Sears said there is no thought at this point\nthat the virus is spreading from person to person and there were no\nreports of influenza among members of either child's family.\n\nThe virus is a strain of influenza A known as H3N2 that has picked up\none of the genes -- the M gene -- of the H1N1 flu strain that caused\nthe 2009 pandemic. The CDC says the swine H3N2 virus probably picked\nup the M gene from the pandemic H1N1 virus when a pig was co-infected\nwith swine H3N2 and the H1N1 strain. It's not clear whether having\nthat gene is helping this virus transmit to humans. But a study done\nin guinea pigs -- one of the animal models for influenza -- showed\nthat the M gene from the 2009 H1N1 virus is critical to the pandemic\nvirus's ability to transmit well [in guinea pigs].\n\nDr. Adolfo Garcia-Sastre, who was involved in that study, said that\nfinding is suggestive, but guinea pigs aren't people. \"Whether these\ncases now reflect that this particular H3N2 with the M gene of the new\nH1N1 transmits better into humans, I think it's too early to say,\"\nsaid Garcia-Sastre, a researcher at New York's Mount Sinai Hospital.\n\nThe swine-origin H3N2 is related -- though not closely - to H3N2\nviruses that circulate each winter among people. The CDC said the H3N2\ncomponent in the annual flu shot would not be expected to protect\nagainst the swine-origin variety. Cox said the virus seems most\nclosely related to human H3N2s that circulated in the early 1990s.\nThat suggests a lot of people would probably have antibodies against\nthis virus, which in turn might make it hard for the virus to be able\nto spread person to person. But people born after the early 1990s\nprobably would not be protected against the virus. Cox said it's not\npossible to be sure, but based on lab work, her team believes fewer\npeople would be susceptible to this virus than were vulnerable to the\n2009 pandemic virus when it emerged.\n\nPigs are highly susceptible to influenza, and a number of flu strains\ncirculate among swine herds. Occasionally, these viruses trigger human\ninfections, generally among people who work with pigs. There have been\n28 such cases reported since 2005, the CDC said.\n\nIn the last few years, the rate of such detections appears to have\nincreased. But no one is sure whether that means there have been more\ncases, or whether there have always been these numbers of cases but\nimprovements to surveillance means they are now being seen.\n\nThe United States and other countries have built up flu surveillance\nefforts in the past 7 or 8 years, 1st due to concern over H5N1 avian\nflu and later as a result of the 2009 pandemic. After a spate of human\ncases of swine H3N2 infection last year [2010], the CDC asked the\nlaboratory that makes candidate viruses for vaccines to make one for a\nswine H3N2 vaccine. That lab is at New York Medical College in\nValhalla. That virus had the same hemagglutinin and neuraminidase\ngenes -- the H and N in a virus's name -- as the new virus does. And\nflu vaccines target [the proteins encoded] in these 2 genes.\n\n[Byline: Helen Branswell]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[There are now 7 cases (6 children and an adult) of infection with an\ninfluenza virus that has been sporadically jumping to people from pigs\nin the United States; 2 in Maine, 3 in Pennsylvania and 2 in Indiana.\nAlthough these cases appear to be independent events, most of those\ninfected had close contact with pigs.\n\nAt present there is no evidence that the virus is spreading from\nperson to person and there has been no report of influenza among\nfamily members.\n\nIt seems likely that the swine H3N2 virus probably picked up its M\ngene (by sub-unit reassortment) from the pandemic H1N1 virus when a\npig was co-infected with swine H3N2 and the H1N1 strain. Model\nexperiments with guinea pigs suggest that the presence of the M gene\nfrom the 2009 H1N pandemic virus favours transmissibility of the the\nH3N2 reassortant virus.\n\nThe infections have been mainly mild, but as a precaution the\ninfluenza division of the CDC has already developed a seed strain for\na vaccine that would protect against this virus and supplied it to\nvaccine producers.\n\nA map of the states of the United States of America can be accessed\nat:\nhttp://www.50states.com/cap.htm. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\nInfluenza (63): USA (ME, NOT NH) swine-origin H3N2 reassortant\n20111102.3260\nInfluenza (60): USA (ME) swine-origin H3N2 reassortant 20111021.3134\nInfluenza (54): (PA) swine-origin H3N2 reassortant, comment\n20110913.2789\nInfluenza (52): (PA), swine-origin H3N2 reassortant, 3 cases\n20110906.2723\nInfluenza (51): swine-origin H3N2 reassortant, children 20110902.2685\nInfluenza (40): H3N2/H1N1 reassortant ex patient "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=958969", "date": "2011-11-05 23:16:17", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (66): UNITED STATES OF AMERICA SWINE-ORIGIN H3N2\nREASSORTANT, UPDATE\n*******************************************************************************\n\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Fri 4 Nov 2011\nSource: The Record.com and the Canadian Press [edited]\nhttp://www.therecord.com/living/article/620441--u-s-spots-two-more-human-cases-of-flu-with-new-swine-virus-but-cases-not-linked\n\n\nMore human cases of flu with new swine virus\n--------------------------------------------\nTwo new cases of human infection with a flu virus that has been\nsporadically jumping to people from pigs have been spotted in the\nUnited States, the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC)\nreported Friday [4 Nov 2011]. The new cases, in Maine and Indiana,\nbring to 7 the number seen in the U.S. since July [2011]. To date\nCanada has seen no infections with this virus, the National\nMicrobiology Laboratory said.\n\nWhile the infections have been mainly mild, the CDC is keeping a close\neye on the situation. The head of the Atlanta-based agency's influenza\ndivision said a seed strain for a vaccine that would protect against\nthis virus has already been developed and has been given to vaccine\nmanufacturers. \"We're really trying to be in front of events in terms\nof preparedness,\" Dr. Nancy Cox said in an interview. \"We're being\nvery vigilant because we realize that there's a portion of the\npopulation ... that really has very little cross-reactive antibodies\nto the swine reassortant virus.\"\n\nThe group Cox was referring to is children. Most of the infections --\n2 from Maine, 3 from Pennsylvania and 2 from Indiana - have been in\nyoung children, though Cox noted one of the cases was a 59-year-old\nperson. Most of the people who have been infected had close contact\nwith pigs and the 2 latest cases are no exception.\n\nDr. Stephen Sears, state epidemiologist for Maine, said the 2 children\nfrom his state who were infected both had a lot of contact with pigs.\nWhile they live in the same region of Maine, the children did not have\ncontact with each other. Sears said there is no thought at this point\nthat the virus is spreading from person to person and there were no\nreports of influenza among members of either child's family.\n\nThe virus is a strain of influenza A known as H3N2 that has picked up\none of the genes -- the M gene -- of the H1N1 flu strain that caused\nthe 2009 pandemic. The CDC says the swine H3N2 virus probably picked\nup the M gene from the pandemic H1N1 virus when a pig was co-infected\nwith swine H3N2 and the H1N1 strain. It's not clear whether having\nthat gene is helping this virus transmit to humans. But a study done\nin guinea pigs -- one of the animal models for influenza -- showed\nthat the M gene from the 2009 H1N1 virus is critical to the pandemic\nvirus's ability to transmit well [in guinea pigs].\n\nDr. Adolfo Garcia-Sastre, who was involved in that study, said that\nfinding is suggestive, but guinea pigs aren't people. \"Whether these\ncases now reflect that this particular H3N2 with the M gene of the new\nH1N1 transmits better into humans, I think it's too early to say,\"\nsaid Garcia-Sastre, a researcher at New York's Mount Sinai Hospital.\n\nThe swine-origin H3N2 is related -- though not closely - to H3N2\nviruses that circulate each winter among people. The CDC said the H3N2\ncomponent in the annual flu shot would not be expected to protect\nagainst the swine-origin variety. Cox said the virus seems most\nclosely related to human H3N2s that circulated in the early 1990s.\nThat suggests a lot of people would probably have antibodies against\nthis virus, which in turn might make it hard for the virus to be able\nto spread person to person. But people born after the early 1990s\nprobably would not be protected against the virus. Cox said it's not\npossible to be sure, but based on lab work, her team believes fewer\npeople would be susceptible to this virus than were vulnerable to the\n2009 pandemic virus when it emerged.\n\nPigs are highly susceptible to influenza, and a number of flu strains\ncirculate among swine herds. Occasionally, these viruses trigger human\ninfections, generally among people who work with pigs. There have been\n28 such cases reported since 2005, the CDC said.\n\nIn the last few years, the rate of such detections appears to have\nincreased. But no one is sure whether that means there have been more\ncases, or whether there have always been these numbers of cases but\nimprovements to surveillance means they are now being seen.\n\nThe United States and other countries have built up flu surveillance\nefforts in the past 7 or 8 years, 1st due to concern over H5N1 avian\nflu and later as a result of the 2009 pandemic. After a spate of human\ncases of swine H3N2 infection last year [2010], the CDC asked the\nlaboratory that makes candidate viruses for vaccines to make one for a\nswine H3N2 vaccine. That lab is at New York Medical College in\nValhalla. That virus had the same hemagglutinin and neuraminidase\ngenes -- the H and N in a virus's name -- as the new virus does. And\nflu vaccines target [the proteins encoded] in these 2 genes.\n\n[Byline: Helen Branswell]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[There are now 7 cases (6 children and an adult) of infection with an\ninfluenza virus that has been sporadically jumping to people from pigs\nin the United States; 2 in Maine, 3 in Pennsylvania and 2 in Indiana.\nAlthough these cases appear to be independent events, most of those\ninfected had close contact with pigs.\n\nAt present there is no evidence that the virus is spreading from\nperson to person and there has been no report of influenza among\nfamily members.\n\nIt seems likely that the swine H3N2 virus probably picked up its M\ngene (by sub-unit reassortment) from the pandemic H1N1 virus when a\npig was co-infected with swine H3N2 and the H1N1 strain. Model\nexperiments with guinea pigs suggest that the presence of the M gene\nfrom the 2009 H1N pandemic virus favours transmissibility of the the\nH3N2 reassortant virus.\n\nThe infections have been mainly mild, but as a precaution the\ninfluenza division of the CDC has already developed a seed strain for\na vaccine that would protect against this virus and supplied it to\nvaccine producers.\n\nA map of the states of the United States of America can be accessed\nat:\nhttp://www.50states.com/cap.htm. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\nInfluenza (63): USA (ME, NOT NH) swine-origin H3N2 reassortant\n20111102.3260\nInfluenza (60): USA (ME) swine-origin H3N2 reassortant 20111021.3134\nInfluenza (54): (PA) swine-origin H3N2 reassortant, comment\n20110913.2789\nInfluenza (52): (PA), swine-origin H3N2 reassortant, 3 cases\n20110906.2723\nInfluenza (51): swine-origin H3N2 reassortant, children 20110902.2685\nInfluenza (40): H3N2/H1N1 reassortant ex patient 20110609.1749\n.................................................cp/ejp/lm", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-03-19 00:00:00", "2021-07-16 00:00:00", The head of the Atlanta-based agency's influenza\ndivision said a seed strain for a vaccine that would protect against\nthis virus has already been developed and has been given to vaccine\nmanufacturers. \"We're really trying to be in front of events in terms\nof preparedness,\" Dr. Nancy Cox said in an interview. \"We're being\nvery vigilant because we realize that there's a portion of the\npopulation ... that really has very little cross-reactive antibodies\nto the swine reassortant virus.\"\n\nThe group Cox was referring to is children. Most of the infections --\n2 from Maine, 3 from Pennsylvania and 2 from Indiana - have been in\nyoung children, though Cox noted one of the cases was a 59-year-old\nperson. Most of the people who have been infected had close contact\nwith pigs and the 2 latest cases are no exception.\n\nDr. Stephen Sears, state epidemiologist for Maine, said the 2 children\nfrom his state who were infected both had a lot of contact with pigs.\nWhile they live in the same region of Maine, the children did not have\ncontact with each other. Sears said there is no thought at this point\nthat the virus is spreading from person to person and there were no\nreports of influenza among members of either child's family.\n\nThe virus is a strain of influenza A known as H3N2 that has picked up\none of the genes -- the M gene -- of the H1N1 flu strain that caused\nthe 2009 pandemic. The CDC says the swine H3N2 virus probably picked\nup the M gene from the pandemic H1N1 virus when a pig was co-infected\nwith swine H3N2 and the H1N1 strain. It's not clear whether having\nthat gene is helping this virus transmit to humans. But a study done\nin guinea pigs -- one of the animal models for influenza -- showed\nthat the M gene from the 2009 H1N1 virus is critical to the pandemic\nvirus's ability to transmit well [in guinea pigs].\n\nDr. Adolfo Garcia-Sastre, who was involved in that study, said that\nfinding is suggestive, but guinea pigs aren't people. \"Whether these\ncases now reflect that this particular H3N2 with the M gene of the new\nH1N1 transmits better into humans, I think it's too early to say,\"\nsaid Garcia-Sastre, a researcher at New York's Mount Sinai Hospital.\n\nThe swine-origin H3N2 is related -- though not closely - to H3N2\nviruses that circulate each winter among people. The CDC said the H3N2\ncomponent in the annual flu shot would not be expected to protect\nagainst the swine-origin variety. Cox said the virus seems most\nclosely related to human H3N2s that circulated in the early 1990s.\nThat suggests a lot of people would probably have antibodies against\nthis virus, which in turn might make it hard for the virus to be able\nto spread person to person. But people born after the early 1990s\nprobably would not be protected against the virus. Cox said it's not\npossible to be sure, but based on lab work, her team believes fewer\npeople would be susceptible to this virus than were vulnerable to the\n2009 pandemic virus when it emerged.\n\nPigs are highly susceptible to influenza, and a number of flu strains\ncirculate among swine herds. Occasionally, these viruses trigger human\ninfections, generally among people who work with pigs. There have been\n28 such cases reported since 2005, the CDC said.\n\nIn the last few years, the rate of such detections appears to have\nincreased. But no one is sure whether that means there have been more\ncases, or whether there have always been these numbers of cases but\nimprovements to surveillance means they are now being seen.\n\nThe United States and other countries have built up flu surveillance\nefforts in the past 7 or 8 years, 1st due to concern over H5N1 avian\nflu and later as a result of the 2009 pandemic. After a spate of human\ncases of swine H3N2 infection last year [2010], the CDC asked the\nlaboratory that makes candidate viruses for vaccines to make one for a\nswine H3N2 vaccine. That lab is at New York Medical College in\nValhalla. That virus had the same hemagglutinin and neuraminidase\ngenes -- the H and N in a virus's name -- as the new virus does. And\nflu vaccines target [the proteins encoded] in these 2 genes.\n\n[Byline: Helen Branswell]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[There are now 7 cases (6 children and an adult) of infection with an\ninfluenza virus that has been sporadically jumping to people from pigs\nin the United States; 2 in Maine, 3 in Pennsylvania and 2 in Indiana.\nAlthough these cases appear to be independent events, most of those\ninfected had close contact with pigs.\n\nAt present there is no evidence that the virus is spreading from\nperson to person and there has been no report of influenza among\nfamily members.\n\nIt seems likely that the swine H3N2 virus probably picked up its M\ngene (by sub-unit reassortment) from the pandemic H1N1 virus when a\npig was co-infected with swine H3N2 and the H1N1 strain. Model\nexperiments with guinea pigs suggest that the presence of the M gene\nfrom the 2009 H1N pandemic virus favours transmissibility of the the\nH3N2 reassortant virus.\n\nThe infections have been mainly mild, but as a precaution the\ninfluenza division of the CDC has already developed a seed strain for\na vaccine that would protect against this virus and supplied it to\nvaccine producers.\n\nA map of the states of the United States of America can be accessed\nat:\nhttp://www.50states.com/cap.htm. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\nInfluenza (63): USA (ME, NOT NH) swine-origin H3N2 virus\n--------------------------------------------\nTwo new cases of human infection with a flu virus that has been\nsporadically jumping to people from pigs have been spotted in the\nUnited States, the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC)\nreported Friday [4 Nov 2011]. The new cases, in Maine and Indiana,\nbring to 7 the number seen in the U.S. since July [2011]. "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=934360", "date": "2010-11-12 18:00:09", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (14): SWINE ORIGIN (TRIPLE REASSORTANT) H3N2 VIRUSES\n**************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Fri 12 Nov 2010\nSource: CDC Online Newsroom [edited]\n<http://www.cdc.gov/media/subtopic/heard.htm#h3n2>\n\n\nReports of human infections with swine origin influenza A (H3N2)\n----------------------------------------------------------------\nThe 12 Nov 2010 FluView reports 2 human infections with swine origin\ninfluenza A (H3N2) viruses in the United States. Test samples from 2\npatients submitted by Wisconsin and Pennsylvania have been confirmed at the\nCDC [Centers for Disease Control and Prevention] as positive for swine\norigin triple-reassortant (tr) H3N2 influenza viruses -- viruses that\nnormally infect pigs. While human infection with swine influenza viruses is\nrare, it can occur. This is most likely to occur when people are in close\nproximity to infected pigs, such as in pig barns and livestock exhibits\nhousing pigs at fairs.\n\nBoth of the patients with confirmed trH3N2 infection reported in FluView\nwere in the vicinity of live pigs. Dates of illness onset in the 2 patients\nare more than 6 weeks apart and the viruses from the 2 patients have some\ngenetic differences, confirming that these 2 cases are not linked. Ongoing\ninvestigations in both states have not shown any evidence of community\ntransmission of these viruses. The most likely scenario at this point is\nthat these are 2 isolated cases of human infection with swine influenza\nviruses that, while very rare, do occur from time to time. Both patients\nhave fully recovered from their illnesses; however, these 2 cases do\nunderscore the importance of human and animal surveillance for influenza.\n\nThese 2 cases reported in FluView bring the total number of human\ninfections with swine origin influenza viruses reported to CDC since 2005\nto 18. Previously, 3 of these reports had been swine origin A (H3N2)\nviruses. The Pennsylvania and Wisconsin cases bring the number of reports\nswine origin A (H3N2) infections in humans in the United States to 5. The\nviruses identified in Pennsylvania and Wisconsin are similar to viruses\nthat infected a patient in Iowa in September 2009, a patient in Kansas in\nAugust 2009 and a patient in Minnesota in May 2010.\n\nSwine Influenza (swine flu) is a respiratory disease of pigs caused by type\nA influenza viruses that regularly causes outbreaks of influenza in pigs.\nSwine flu viruses cause high levels of illness and low death rates in pigs.\nSwine influenza viruses may circulate among swine throughout the year, but\nmost outbreaks occur during the late fall and winter months similar to\noutbreaks in humans. There are 4 main influenza type A virus subtypes that\nhave been isolated in pigs: H1N1, H1N2, H3N2, and H3N1. Most flu viruses\ncirculating in pigs are referred to as \"triple-reassortant\" viruses because\nthese flu viruses contain genes from human, swine and avian influenza viruses.\n\nMost commonly, cases of human infection with swine-origin influenza viruses\noccur in people with direct exposure to pigs. The patient in Pennsylvania\nlives in an area where live pigs are farmed and the patient in Wisconsin\nbecame sick 2 days after attending a state fair where pigs were exhibited.\nIt's important to note that swine influenza viruses are not transmitted to\nhumans by food. You can not get swine influenza from eating pork or pork\nproducts. Eating properly handled and cooked pork and pork products is safe.\n\nIn the past, CDC received reports of approximately one human infection with\na swine influenza virus every one to 2 years, but in the past few years,\nabout 3 cases have been reported per year. Increased reporting of human\ninfections with swine influenza could be the result of increased influenza\ntesting capacity and capabilities in public health laboratories.\n\nThese trH3N2 viruses are different from the 2009 H1N1 virus that has been\ncirculating in the United States since late April 2009. They are also\ndifferent from human seasonal influenza A (H3N2) viruses that typically\ncirculate among people during the flu season. Swine trH3N2 viruses commonly\ncirculate in pigs in North America, but rarely infect humans. These viruses\nare different from the swine classical H1N1 or swine trH1N1 influenza\nviruses that also circulate in pigs in North America because they have H3N2\nsurface antigens. Tr H3N2 viruses first emerged in North American swine\nherds in the late 1990s. The H3 and N2 genes which 1st emerged in swine flu\nviruses originated from human seasonal H3N2 influenza viruses that\ncirculated globally among humans in the late 1990s.\n\nAlthough the vast majority of instances of human infection with animal\ninfluenza viruses do not result in human to human transmission, each case\nshould be fully investigated to be sure that such viruses are not spreading\namong humans and to limit further exposure of humans to infected animals if\ninfected animals are identified. Surveillance for both seasonal and novel\ninfluenza viruses is conducted by the CDC and its state and local health\npartners year round.\n\nFor more information about swine influenza, see:\n<http://www.cdc.gov/flu/swineflu/>.\nWeekly US surveillance updates are published in FluView and posted at:\n<http://www.cdc.gov/flu/weekly/fluactivitysurv.htm>.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Mary Marshall\n\n[There are 4 main influenza type A virus subtypes that have been isolated\nin pigs: H1N1, H1N2, H3N2, and H3N1. Most flu viruses circulating in pigs\nare referred to as \"triple-reassortant\" viruses because these flu viruses\ncontain genes from human, swine, and avian influenza viruses.\n\nSwine triple-reassortant H3N2 influenza viruses commonly circulate in pigs\nin North America, but rarely infect humans. The total number of human\ninfections with swine origin influenza viruses reported to the CDC since\n2005 had been 16, and 3 of these reports had been swine origin A (H3N2)\nviruses. The Pennsylvania and Wisconsin cases described above bring the\nnumber of reports swine origin A (H3N2) infections in humans in the United\nStates to 5, and the total of swine origin A influenza A viruses to 18.\n\nThe dates of illness onset in the 2 patients are more than 6 weeks apart\nand the viruses from the 2 patients have some genetic differences,\nconfirming that these 2 cases are not linked.\n\nSwine flu viruses cause high levels of illness and low death rates in pigs.\nSwine influenza viruses may circulate among swine throughout the year, but\nmost outbreaks occur during the late fall and winter months similar to\noutbreaks in humans.\n\nTransmission of swine (tr) influenza a viruses to humans has been rare,\neven in individuals occupationally exposed to pigs. With the exception of\nthe swine-origin A (H1N1) 2009 pandemic virus human-to-human transmission\nhas occurred rarely, if at all.\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of the United States, showing the\nlocations of the states of Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, can be accessed at\n<http://healthmap.org/r/01bQ>. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\nInfluenza (13): WHO update 20101111.4092\n\n...................cp/ejp/sh\n\n\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. These viruses\nare different from the swine classical H1N1 or swine trH1N1 influenza\nviruses that also circulate in pigs in North America because they have H3N2\nsurface antigens. Tr H3N2 viruses first emerged in North American swine\nherds in the late 1990s. The H3 and N2 genes which 1st emerged in swine flu\nviruses originated from human seasonal H3N2 influenza viruses that\ncirculated globally among humans in the late 1990s.\n\nAlthough the vast majority of instances of human infection with animal\ninfluenza viruses do not result in human to human transmission, each case\nshould be fully investigated to be sure that such viruses are not spreading\namong humans and to limit further exposure of humans to infected animals if\ninfected animals are identified. Surveillance for both seasonal and novel\ninfluenza viruses is conducted by the CDC and its state and local health\npartners year round.\n\nFor more information about swine influenza, see:\n<http://www.cdc.gov/flu/swineflu/>.\nWeekly US surveillance updates are published in FluView and posted at:\n<http://www.cdc.gov/flu/weekly/fluactivitysurv.htm>.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Mary Marshall\n\n[There are 4 main influenza type A virus subtypes that have been isolated\nin pigs: H1N1, H1N2, H3N2, and H3N1. Most flu viruses circulating in pigs\nare referred to as \"triple-reassortant\" viruses because these flu viruses\ncontain genes from human, swine, and avian influenza viruses.\n\nSwine triple-reassortant H3N2 influenza viruses commonly circulate in pigs\nin North America, but rarely infect humans. The total number of human\ninfections with swine origin influenza viruses reported to the CDC since\n2005 had been 16, and 3 of these reports had been swine origin A (H3N2)\nviruses. The Pennsylvania and Wisconsin cases described above bring the\nnumber of reports swine origin A (H3N2) infections in humans in the United\nStates to 5, and the total of swine origin A influenza A viruses to 18.\n\nThe dates of illness onset in the 2 patients are more than 6 weeks apart\nand the viruses from the 2 patients have some genetic differences,\nconfirming that these 2 cases are not linked.\n\nSwine flu viruses cause high levels of illness and low death rates in pigs.\nSwine influenza viruses may circulate among swine throughout the year, but\nmost outbreaks occur during the late fall and winter months similar to\noutbreaks in humans.\n\nTransmission of swine (tr) influenza a viruses to humans has been rare,\neven in individuals occupationally exposed to pigs. With the exception of\nthe swine-origin A (H1N1) 2009 pandemic virus human-to-human transmission\nhas occurred rarely, if at all.\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of the United States, showing the\nlocations of the states of Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, can be accessed at\n<http://healthmap.org/r/01bQ>. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\nInfluenza (13): WHO update 20101111.4092\n\n...................cp/ejp/sh\n\n\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Test samples from 2\npatients submitted by Wisconsin and Pennsylvania have been confirmed at the\nCDC [Centers for Disease Control and Prevention] as positive for swine\norigin triple-reassortant (tr) H3N2 influenza viruses -- viruses that\nnormally infect pigs. While human infection with swine influenza viruses is\nrare, it can occur. This is most likely to occur when people are in close\nproximity to infected pigs, such as in pig barns and livestock exhibits\nhousing pigs at fairs.\n\nBoth of the patients with confirmed trH3N2 infection reported in FluView\nwere in the vicinity of live pigs. Dates of illness onset in the 2 patients\nare more than 6 weeks apart and the viruses from the 2 patients have some\ngenetic differences, confirming that these 2 cases are not linked. Ongoing\ninvestigations in both states have not shown any evidence of community\ntransmission of these viruses. The most likely scenario at this point is\nthat these are 2 isolated cases of human infection with swine influenza\nviruses that, while very rare, do occur from time to time. Both patients\nhave fully recovered from their illnesses; however, these 2 cases do\nunderscore the importance of human and animal surveillance for influenza.\n\nThese 2 cases reported in FluView bring the total number of human\ninfections with swine origin influenza viruses reported to CDC since 2005\nto 18. Previously, 3 of these reports had been swine origin A (H3N2)\nviruses. The Pennsylvania and Wisconsin cases bring the number of reports\nswine origin A (H3N2) infections in humans in the United States to 5. The\nviruses identified in Pennsylvania and Wisconsin are similar to viruses\nthat infected a patient in Iowa in September 2009, a patient in Kansas in\nAugust 2009 and a patient in Minnesota in May 2010.\n\nSwine Influenza (swine flu) is a respiratory disease of pigs caused by type\nA influenza viruses that regularly causes outbreaks of influenza in pigs.\nSwine flu viruses cause high levels of illness and low death rates in pigs.\nSwine influenza viruses may circulate among swine throughout the year, but\nmost outbreaks occur during the late fall and winter months similar to\noutbreaks in humans. There are 4 main influenza type A virus subtypes that\nhave been isolated in pigs: H1N1, H1N2, H3N2, and H3N1. Most flu viruses\ncirculating in pigs are referred to as \"triple-reassortant\" viruses because\nthese flu viruses contain genes from human, swine and avian influenza viruses.\n\nMost commonly, cases of human infection with swine-origin influenza viruses\noccur in people with direct exposure to pigs. The patient in Pennsylvania\nlives in an area where live pigs are farmed and the patient in Wisconsin\nbecame sick 2 days after attending a state fair where pigs were exhibited.\nIt's important to note that swine influenza viruses are not transmitted to\nhumans by food. You can not get swine influenza from eating pork or pork\nproducts. Eating properly handled and cooked pork and pork products is safe.\n\nIn the past, CDC received reports of approximately one human infection with\na swine influenza virus every one to 2 years, but in the past few years,\nabout 3 cases have been reported per year. Increased reporting of human\ninfections with swine influenza could be the result of increased influenza\ntesting capacity and capabilities in public health laboratories.\n\nThese trH3N2 viruses are different from the 2009 H1N1 virus that has been\ncirculating in the United States since late April 2009. They are also\ndifferent from human seasonal influenza A (H3N2) viruses that typically\ncirculate among people during the flu season. Swine trH3N2 viruses commonly\ncirculate in pigs in North America, but rarely infect humans. These viruses\nare different from the swine classical H1N1 or swine trH1N1 influenza\nviruses that also circulate in pigs in North America because they have H3N2\nsurface antigens. Tr H3N2 viruses first emerged in North American swine\nherds in the late 1990s. The H3 and N2 genes which 1st emerged in swine flu\nviruses originated from human seasonal H3N2 influenza viruses that\ncirculated globally among humans in the late 1990s.\n\nAlthough the vast majority of instances of human infection with animal\ninfluenza viruses do not result in human to human transmission, each case\nshould be fully investigated to be sure that such viruses are not spreading\namong humans and to limit further exposure of humans to infected animals if\ninfected animals are identified. Surveillance for both seasonal and novel\ninfluenza viruses is conducted by the CDC and its state and local health\npartners year round.\n\nFor more information about swine influenza, see:\n<http://www.cdc.gov/flu/swineflu/>.\nWeekly US surveillance updates are published in FluView and posted at:\n<http://www.cdc.gov/flu/weekly/fluactivitysurv.htm>.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Mary Marshall\n\n[There are 4 main influenza type A virus subtypes that have been isolated\nin pigs: H1N1, H1N2, H3N2, and H3N1. Most flu viruses circulating in pigs\nare referred to as \"triple-reassortant\" viruses because these flu viruses\ncontain genes from human, swine, and avian influenza viruses.\n\nSwine triple-reassortant H3N2 influenza viruses commonly circulate in pigs\nin North America, but rarely infect humans. The total number of human\ninfections with swine origin influenza viruses reported to the CDC since\n2005 had been 16, and 3 of these reports had been swine origin A (H3N2)\nviruses. The Pennsylvania and Wisconsin cases described above bring the\nnumber of reports swine origin A (H3N2) infections in humans in the United\nStates to 5, and the total of swine origin A influenza A viruses to 18.\n\nThe dates of illness onset in the 2 patients are more than 6 weeks apart\nand the viruses from the 2 patients have some genetic differences,\nconfirming that these 2 cases are not linked.\n\nSwine flu viruses cause high levels of illness and low death rates in pigs.\nSwine influenza viruses may circulate among swine throughout the year, but\nmost outbreaks occur during the late fall and winter months similar to\noutbreaks in humans.\n\nTransmission of swine (tr) influenza a viruses to humans has been rare,\neven in individuals occupationally exposed to pigs. With the exception of\nthe swine-origin A (H1N1) 2009 pandemic virus human-to-human transmission\nhas occurred rarely, if at all.\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of the United States, showing the\nlocations of the states of Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, can be accessed at\n<http://healthmap.org/r/01bQ>. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\nInfluenza (13): WHO update 20101111.4092\n\n...................cp/ejp/sh\n\n\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed.
