The Pennsylvania and Wisconsin cases described above bring the\nnumber of reports swine origin A (H3N2) infections in humans in the United\nStates to 5, and the total of swine origin A influenza A viruses to 18.\n\nThe dates of illness onset in the 2 patients are more than 6 weeks apart\nand the viruses from the 2 patients have some genetic differences,\nconfirming that these 2 cases are not linked.\n\nSwine flu viruses cause high levels of illness and low death rates in pigs.\nSwine influenza viruses may circulate among swine throughout the year, but\nmost outbreaks occur during the late fall and winter months similar to\noutbreaks in humans.\n\nTransmission of swine (tr) influenza a viruses to humans has been rare,\neven in individuals occupationally exposed to pigs. With the exception of\nthe swine-origin A (H1N1) 2009 pandemic virus human-to-human transmission\nhas occurred rarely, if at all.\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of the United States, showing the\nlocations of the states of Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, can be accessed at\n<http://healthmap.org/r/01bQ>. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\nInfluenza (13): WHO update 20101111.4092\n\n...................cp/ejp/sh\n\n\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Korea", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=775153", "date": "2010-11-12 18:00:09", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (14): SWINE ORIGIN (TRIPLE REASSORTANT) H3N2 VIRUSES\n**************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Fri 12 Nov 2010\nSource: CDC Online Newsroom [edited]\n<http://www.cdc.gov/media/subtopic/heard.htm#h3n2>\n\n\nReports of human infections with swine origin influenza A (H3N2)\n----------------------------------------------------------------\nThe 12 Nov 2010 FluView reports 2 human infections with swine origin\ninfluenza A (H3N2) viruses in the United States. Test samples from 2\npatients submitted by Wisconsin and Pennsylvania have been confirmed at the\nCDC [Centers for Disease Control and Prevention] as positive for swine\norigin triple-reassortant (tr) H3N2 influenza viruses -- viruses that\nnormally infect pigs. While human infection with swine influenza viruses is\nrare, it can occur. This is most likely to occur when people are in close\nproximity to infected pigs, such as in pig barns and livestock exhibits\nhousing pigs at fairs.\n\nBoth of the patients with confirmed trH3N2 infection reported in FluView\nwere in the vicinity of live pigs. Dates of illness onset in the 2 patients\nare more than 6 weeks apart and the viruses from the 2 patients have some\ngenetic differences, confirming that these 2 cases are not linked. Ongoing\ninvestigations in both states have not shown any evidence of community\ntransmission of these viruses. The most likely scenario at this point is\nthat these are 2 isolated cases of human infection with swine influenza\nviruses that, while very rare, do occur from time to time. Both patients\nhave fully recovered from their illnesses; however, these 2 cases do\nunderscore the importance of human and animal surveillance for influenza.\n\nThese 2 cases reported in FluView bring the total number of human\ninfections with swine origin influenza viruses reported to CDC since 2005\nto 18. Previously, 3 of these reports had been swine origin A (H3N2)\nviruses. The Pennsylvania and Wisconsin cases bring the number of reports\nswine origin A (H3N2) infections in humans in the United States to 5. The\nviruses identified in Pennsylvania and Wisconsin are similar to viruses\nthat infected a patient in Iowa in September 2009, a patient in Kansas in\nAugust 2009 and a patient in Minnesota in May 2010.\n\nSwine Influenza (swine flu) is a respiratory disease of pigs caused by type\nA influenza viruses that regularly causes outbreaks of influenza in pigs.\nSwine flu viruses cause high levels of illness and low death rates in pigs.\nSwine influenza viruses may circulate among swine throughout the year, but\nmost outbreaks occur during the late fall and winter months similar to\noutbreaks in humans. There are 4 main influenza type A virus subtypes that\nhave been isolated in pigs: H1N1, H1N2, H3N2, and H3N1. Most flu viruses\ncirculating in pigs are referred to as \"triple-reassortant\" viruses because\nthese flu viruses contain genes from human, swine and avian influenza viruses.\n\nMost commonly, cases of human infection with swine-origin influenza viruses\noccur in people with direct exposure to pigs. The patient in Pennsylvania\nlives in an area where live pigs are farmed and the patient in Wisconsin\nbecame sick 2 days after attending a state fair where pigs were exhibited.\nIt's important to note that swine influenza viruses are not transmitted to\nhumans by food. You can not get swine influenza from eating pork or pork\nproducts. Eating properly handled and cooked pork and pork products is safe.\n\nIn the past, CDC received reports of approximately one human infection with\na swine influenza virus every one to 2 years, but in the past few years,\nabout 3 cases have been reported per year. Increased reporting of human\ninfections with swine influenza could be the result of increased influenza\ntesting capacity and capabilities in public health laboratories.\n\nThese trH3N2 viruses are different from the 2009 H1N1 virus that has been\ncirculating in the United States since late April 2009. They are also\ndifferent from human seasonal influenza A (H3N2) viruses that typically\ncirculate among people during the flu season. Swine trH3N2 viruses commonly\ncirculate in pigs in North America, but rarely infect humans. These viruses\nare different from the swine classical H1N1 or swine trH1N1 influenza\nviruses that also circulate in pigs in North America because they have H3N2\nsurface antigens. Tr H3N2 viruses first emerged in North American swine\nherds in the late 1990s. The H3 and N2 genes which 1st emerged in swine flu\nviruses originated from human seasonal H3N2 influenza viruses that\ncirculated globally among humans in the late 1990s.\n\nAlthough the vast majority of instances of human infection with animal\ninfluenza viruses do not result in human to human transmission, each case\nshould be fully investigated to be sure that such viruses are not spreading\namong humans and to limit further exposure of humans to infected animals if\ninfected animals are identified. Surveillance for both seasonal and novel\ninfluenza viruses is conducted by the CDC and its state and local health\npartners year round.\n\nFor more information about swine influenza, see:\n<http://www.cdc.gov/flu/swineflu/>.\nWeekly US surveillance updates are published in FluView and posted at:\n<http://www.cdc.gov/flu/weekly/fluactivitysurv.htm>.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Mary Marshall\n\n[There are 4 main influenza type A virus subtypes that have been isolated\nin pigs: H1N1, H1N2, H3N2, and H3N1. Most flu viruses circulating in pigs\nare referred to as \"triple-reassortant\" viruses because these flu viruses\ncontain genes from human, swine, and avian influenza viruses.\n\nSwine triple-reassortant H3N2 influenza viruses commonly circulate in pigs\nin North America, but rarely infect humans. The total number of human\ninfections with swine origin influenza viruses reported to the CDC since\n2005 had been 16, and 3 of these reports had been swine origin A (H3N2)\nviruses. The Pennsylvania and Wisconsin cases described above bring the\nnumber of reports swine origin A (H3N2) infections in humans in the United\nStates to 5, and the total of swine origin A influenza A viruses to 18.\n\nThe dates of illness onset in the 2 patients are more than 6 weeks apart\nand the viruses from the 2 patients have some genetic differences,\nconfirming that these 2 cases are not linked.\n\nSwine flu viruses cause high levels of illness and low death rates in pigs.\nSwine influenza viruses may circulate among swine throughout the year, but\nmost outbreaks occur during the late fall and winter months similar to\noutbreaks in humans.\n\nTransmission of swine (tr) influenza a viruses to humans has been rare,\neven in individuals occupationally exposed to pigs. With the exception of\nthe swine-origin A (H1N1) 2009 pandemic virus human-to-human transmission\nhas occurred rarely, if at all.\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of the United States, showing the\nlocations of the states of Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, can be accessed at\n<http://healthmap.org/r/01bQ>. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\nInfluenza (13): WHO update 20101111.4092\n\n...................cp/ejp/sh\n\n\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. The most likely source of the virus is a swine herd in\nthe area, he said.\nThe flock was late in its laying cycle when one house experienced a\ndrop in egg production. The flock processed shortly thereafter.\nOn 14 Jan 2005, the National Veterinary Services Laboratory confirmed\nthat the virus isolated from the flock at Rollins laboratory was a\nsubtype H3N2 influenza virus. This diagnosis has significance for\nthose that export to the Russian Federation, said state veterinary\nofficials. The export agreements made with Russia include the\nrequirement to report all subtypes of influenza diagnosed in\ncommercial poultry.\nThe isolation of H3N2 influenza from North Carolina poultry will be\nreported by USDA to Russia. This will change North Carolina's Avian\nInfluenza status as it relates to Russian exports from A to B for the\nnext 30 days. After 30 days has passed the status will change to C.\nIt will be an additional 5 months before the status returns to A.\nWhile in status B, processing plants located in the index county and\nadjacent counties will not be able to export to Russia. The counties\naffected include Sampson, Harnett, Johnston, Wayne, Duplin, Pender,\nBladen and Cumberland.\nAll other processing plants in the state exporting to Russia will\nneed to test 15 birds per flock for the virus at slaughter during the\n30-day period if they wish to export to Russia. If the plant has no\nintention of exporting product produced during the 30-day period they\ndo not need to test.\nWhile in status C, processing plants located in the noted counties\ncan resume exports to Russia if they test 15 birds per flock at\nslaughter. Processing plants located outside of the index county and\nadjacent counties can discontinue testing for avian influenza.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[H3N2 is considered a low-pathogenicity variety. It also appears that\nthis outbreak will be managed without entire flock depopulation,\nexcept for the flock of origin. - Mod.TG]\nSee Also\n2004\n----\nAvian influenza, H3N2, poultry - USA (MO) 20040916.2577\n..................tg/pg/lm\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. The most likely source of the virus is a swine herd in\nthe area, he said.\nThe flock was late in its laying cycle when one house experienced a\ndrop in egg production. The flock processed shortly thereafter.\nOn 14 Jan 2005, the National Veterinary Services Laboratory confirmed\nthat the virus isolated from the flock at Rollins laboratory was a\nsubtype H3N2 influenza virus. This diagnosis has significance for\nthose that export to the Russian Federation, said state veterinary\nofficials. The export agreements made with Russia include the\nrequirement to report all subtypes of influenza diagnosed in\ncommercial poultry.\nThe isolation of H3N2 influenza from North Carolina poultry will be\nreported by USDA to Russia. This will change North Carolina's Avian\nInfluenza status as it relates to Russian exports from A to B for the\nnext 30 days. After 30 days has passed the status will change to C.\nIt will be an additional 5 months before the status returns to A.\nWhile in status B, processing plants located in the index county and\nadjacent counties will not be able to export to Russia. The counties\naffected include Sampson, Harnett, Johnston, Wayne, Duplin, Pender,\nBladen and Cumberland.\nAll other processing plants in the state exporting to Russia will\nneed to test 15 birds per flock for the virus at slaughter during the\n30-day period if they wish to export to Russia. If the plant has no\nintention of exporting product produced during the 30-day period they\ndo not need to test.\nWhile in status C, processing plants located in the noted counties\ncan resume exports to Russia if they test 15 birds per flock at\nslaughter. Processing plants located outside of the index county and\nadjacent counties can discontinue testing for avian influenza.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[H3N2 is considered a low-pathogenicity variety. It also appears that\nthis outbreak will be managed without entire flock depopulation,\nexcept for the flock of origin. - Mod.TG]\nSee Also\n2004\n----\nAvian influenza, H3N2, poultry - USA (MO) 20040916.2577\n..................tg/pg/lm\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. - CHINA (HONG KONG)\n*************************************************\nA ProMED post\n<http://www.healthnet.org/programs/promed.html>\nSee Also\nInfluenzavirus A (H3N2): antigenic variations 980118215821\nInfluenzaviruses, porcine intermediation 980830110724\nInfluenza H5N1, human - China (Hong Kong) 980110230837\nInfluenza, bird-to-man, first case? 970820145405\nInfluenza, bird-to-human - China (Hong Kong) (38) 980210225824\nDate: Wed, 20 Oct 1999 21:55:53 -0200\nFrom: ProMED <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: South China Morning Post, Tue 19 Oct 1999\n\nA 10-month-old girl has been struck down by \"swine flu\", in the first case\nof its kind in Hong Kong and only the third known infection from pigs\nworldwide.\nExperts at the United States Centres for Disease Control in Atlanta are\ninvestigating, as the Hong Kong Department of Health tries to find out how\nthe girl who had no contact with pigs became infected.\nThe girl was admitted to Tuen Mun Hospital last month and discharged after\ntreatment for an upper respiratory infection; her unusual condition did not\nemerge for several weeks.\nDuring her hospital stay, a nasal swab was taken for testing by the\nDepartment of Health, which on Tuesday confirmed that she had the influenza\nH3N2 virus. The highly infectious human strains of influenza, Sydney and\nHarbin flu viruses, are also H3N2, but in this case swab tests revealed the\ngirl did not catch the bug from another person.\n\"The initial analysis suggests that this virus may be of swine origin and\ntherefore we are carrying out further investigations,\" department\nconsultant on community medicine Dr Mak Kwok-hang said.\n\"According to her family members, this baby girl has had no history of\ncontact with pigs.\" A Tuen Mun Hospital spokeswoman said the girl, an only\nchild, had been discharged in good health on 10 Sep 1999, two days after\nbeing admitted to the paediatrics ward with a cough and fever. She had not\nbeen ill since and had not been readmitted to hospital. The girl's\nparents, who live in a Tuen Mun public housing estate, have not been\nunwell, and have tested negative for the H3N2 flu virus.\nDr Mak said H3N2 had been transmitted between humans for decades, and had\nbeen passed from people to pigs. In this case, pigs had passed it back to a\nhuman.\nOnly two other cases of such transmission have been reported in unrelated\ncases in the Netherlands in 1993, a boy and a girl were diagnosed with H3N2\npicked up from pigs.\nThe boy's father worked on a pig farm, but the girl's family reported no\nconnection with pigs. Symptoms again were mild and the children recovered\nwithout infecting family members.\nDr Mak said this particular H3N2 did not appear to be particularly\ninfectious, and said comparisons with the H5N1 bird flu outbreak of 1997\nwere \"unfair\", as this virus was not a new disease.\n\"We're of the opinion that this present case is a scientific discovery\nwhich does not appear to pose any major threat to public health.\" He said\nflu was picked up by breathing in airborne secretions from an infected\nperson or animal, and was not food-borne.\n\"There's no risk of getting influenza by eating pork,\" he said. Hong Kong\nMedical Association infectious diseases specialist Dr Lo Wing-lok said he\nwas not worried by the Hong Kong girl's case. \"In fact, I'm encouraged,\nbecause this shows that our surveillance system is very sensitive,\" he said.\nEpidemics humans picked up and passed between each other a disease\npreviously found only in animals, Dr Lo said. \"I don't think it's very\nlikely to happen in this case,\" he said.\nBoth Dr Lo and Dr Mak said health care workers needed to be vigilant and\nwatch out for any further cases. The results of more tests on the sample\nsent to the US are expected by the end of the week. In 1997, six people in\nHong Kong died of \"bird flu\", the H5N1 strain of influenza previously known\nonly in birds.\n[Byline: Jo Bowman\n*********\nCOMMENT\nAs the article suggests, because of intensive surveillance in Hong Kong,\ntransmission to humans of avian and porcine influenza A viruses is likely\nto be documented with increasing frequency. Hong Kong's H5N1 avian\ninfluenza outbreak two years ago caused international alarm, and led to\nexpanded surveillance for human and zoonotic influenza A circulation.\nAlthough human infection with porcine influenza viruses has long been\nknown, the implications for viral evolution and development of pathogenic\npotential are incompletely understood. In addition to H1N1 viruses, H3N2\nviruses also circulate in pigs. Pig to human and human to pig H3N2\ntransmission have both been documented, and it has long been thought that\npigs may play a role in the generation of new human strains. As pigs may\nbe infected with human, pig, and avian influenza strains, they are a\npotential source of new viral reassortants. Pigs, for example, might play\na catalytic role in \"transforming\" avian viral strains into human viral\nequivalents. The Hong Kong newspaper report does not suggest that this was\nthe case, however.\nImportant additional questions include whether pig to human transmission is\ncommon, whether clinically apparent human cases are the exception to the\nrule, and whether such instances are background noise, or constitute a\npotential risk for emergence of a new pandemic viruses. Based on the\ninformation provided, Dr. Mak's comment that there seems to be little human\nthreat seems reasonable. That CDC is assisting in the investigation is\nalso good news. This and recent influenza events provide continuing\nopportunities to learn more, via coordinated epidemiologic and viral\ngenetic study, about critical aspects of human-zoonotic influenza virus\nrelationships, leading to a better understanding of where human viruses\ncome from and how to control and prevent them.\n--\nDavid M. Hong Kong\nMedical Association infectious diseases specialist Dr Lo Wing-lok said he\nwas not worried by the Hong Kong girl's case. \"In fact, I'm encouraged,\nbecause this shows that our surveillance system is very sensitive,\" he said.\nEpidemics humans picked up and passed between each other a disease\npreviously found only in animals, Dr Lo said. \"I don't think it's very\nlikely to happen in this case,\" he said.\nBoth Dr Lo and Dr Mak said health care workers needed to be vigilant and\nwatch out for any further cases. The results of more tests on the sample\nsent to the US are expected by the end of the week. In 1997, six people in\nHong Kong died of \"bird flu\", the H5N1 strain of influenza previously known\nonly in birds.\n[Byline: Jo Bowman\n*********\nCOMMENT\nAs the article suggests, because of intensive surveillance in Hong Kong,\ntransmission to humans of avian and porcine influenza A viruses is likely\nto be documented with increasing frequency. Hong Kong's H5N1 avian\ninfluenza outbreak two years ago caused international alarm, and led to\nexpanded surveillance for human and zoonotic influenza A circulation.\nAlthough human infection with porcine influenza viruses has long been\nknown, the implications for viral evolution and development of pathogenic\npotential are incompletely understood. In addition to H1N1 viruses, H3N2\nviruses also circulate in pigs. Pig to human and human to pig H3N2\ntransmission have both been documented, and it has long been thought that\npigs may play a role in the generation of new human strains. As pigs may\nbe infected with human, pig, and avian influenza strains, they are a\npotential source of new viral reassortants. Pigs, for example, might play\na catalytic role in \"transforming\" avian viral strains into human viral\nequivalents. The Hong Kong newspaper report does not suggest that this was\nthe case, however.\nImportant additional questions include whether pig to human transmission is\ncommon, whether clinically apparent human cases are the exception to the\nrule, and whether such instances are background noise, or constitute a\npotential risk for emergence of a new pandemic viruses. Based on the\ninformation provided, Dr. Mak's comment that there seems to be little human\nthreat seems reasonable. That CDC is assisting in the investigation is\nalso good news. This and recent influenza events provide continuing\nopportunities to learn more, via coordinated epidemiologic and viral\ngenetic study, about critical aspects of human-zoonotic influenza virus\nrelationships, leading to a better understanding of where human viruses\ncome from and how to control and prevent them.\n--\nDavid M. - CHINA (HONG KONG)\n*************************************************\nA ProMED post\n<http://www.healthnet.org/programs/promed.html>\nSee Also\nInfluenzavirus A (H3N2): antigenic variations 980118215821\nInfluenzaviruses, porcine intermediation 980830110724\nInfluenza H5N1, human - China (Hong Kong) 980110230837\nInfluenza, bird-to-man, first case? 970820145405\nInfluenza, bird-to-human - China (Hong Kong) (38) 980210225824\nDate: Wed, 20 Oct 1999 21:55:53 -0200\nFrom: ProMED <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: South China Morning Post, Tue 19 Oct 1999\n\nA 10-month-old girl has been struck down by \"swine flu\", in the first case\nof its kind in Hong Kong and only the third known infection from pigs\nworldwide.\nExperts at the United States Centres for Disease Control in Atlanta are\ninvestigating, as the Hong Kong Department of Health tries to find out how\nthe girl who had no contact with pigs became infected.\nThe girl was admitted to Tuen Mun Hospital last month and discharged after\ntreatment for an upper respiratory infection; her unusual condition did not\nemerge for several weeks.\nDuring her hospital stay, a nasal swab was taken for testing by the\nDepartment of Health, which on Tuesday confirmed that she had the influenza\nH3N2 virus. The highly infectious human strains of influenza, Sydney and\nHarbin flu viruses, are also H3N2, but in this case swab tests revealed the\ngirl did not catch the bug from another person.\n\"The initial analysis suggests that this virus may be of swine origin and\ntherefore we are carrying out further investigations,\" department\nconsultant on community medicine Dr Mak Kwok-hang said.\n\"According to her family members, this baby girl has had no history of\ncontact with pigs.\" A Tuen Mun Hospital spokeswoman said the girl, an only\nchild, had been discharged in good health on 10 Sep 1999, two days after\nbeing admitted to the paediatrics ward with a cough and fever. She had not\nbeen ill since and had not been readmitted to hospital. The girl's\nparents, who live in a Tuen Mun public housing estate, have not been\nunwell, and have tested negative for the H3N2 flu virus.\nDr Mak said H3N2 had been transmitted between humans for decades, and had\nbeen passed from people to pigs. In this case, pigs had passed it back to a\nhuman.\nOnly two other cases of such transmission have been reported in unrelated\ncases in the Netherlands in 1993, a boy and a girl were diagnosed with H3N2\npicked up from pigs.\nThe boy's father worked on a pig farm, but the girl's family reported no\nconnection with pigs. Symptoms again were mild and the children recovered\nwithout infecting family members.\nDr Mak said this particular H3N2 did not appear to be particularly\ninfectious, and said comparisons with the H5N1 bird flu outbreak of 1997\nwere \"unfair\", as this virus was not a new disease.\n\"We're of the opinion that this present case is a scientific discovery\nwhich does not appear to pose any major threat to public health.\" He said\nflu was picked up by breathing in airborne secretions from an infected\nperson or animal, and was not food-borne.\n\"There's no risk of getting influenza by eating pork,\" he said. Hong Kong\nMedical Association infectious diseases specialist Dr Lo Wing-lok said he\nwas not worried by the Hong Kong girl's case. \"In fact, I'm encouraged,\nbecause this shows that our surveillance system is very sensitive,\" he said.\nEpidemics humans picked up and passed between each other a disease\npreviously found only in animals, Dr Lo said. \"I don't think it's very\nlikely to happen in this case,\" he said.\nBoth Dr Lo and Dr Mak said health care workers needed to be vigilant and\nwatch out for any further cases. The results of more tests on the sample\nsent to the US are expected by the end of the week. In 1997, six people in\nHong Kong died of \"bird flu\", the H5N1 strain of influenza previously known\nonly in birds.\n[Byline: Jo Bowman\n*********\nCOMMENT\nAs the article suggests, because of intensive surveillance in Hong Kong,\ntransmission to humans of avian and porcine influenza A viruses is likely\nto be documented with increasing frequency. Hong Kong's H5N1 avian\ninfluenza outbreak two years ago caused international alarm, and led to\nexpanded surveillance for human and zoonotic influenza A circulation.\nAlthough human infection with porcine influenza viruses has long been\nknown, the implications for viral evolution and development of pathogenic\npotential are incompletely understood. In addition to H1N1 viruses, H3N2\nviruses also circulate in pigs. Pig to human and human to pig H3N2\ntransmission have both been documented, and it has long been thought that\npigs may play a role in the generation of new human strains. As pigs may\nbe infected with human, pig, and avian influenza strains, they are a\npotential source of new viral reassortants. Pigs, for example, might play\na catalytic role in \"transforming\" avian viral strains into human viral\nequivalents. The Hong Kong newspaper report does not suggest that this was\nthe case, however.\nImportant additional questions include whether pig to human transmission is\ncommon, whether clinically apparent human cases are the exception to the\nrule, and whether such instances are background noise, or constitute a\npotential risk for emergence of a new pandemic viruses. Based on the\ninformation provided, Dr. Mak's comment that there seems to be little human\nthreat seems reasonable. That CDC is assisting in the investigation is\nalso good news. This and recent influenza events provide continuing\nopportunities to learn more, via coordinated epidemiologic and viral\ngenetic study, about critical aspects of human-zoonotic influenza virus\nrelationships, leading to a better understanding of where human viruses\ncome from and how to control and prevent them.\n--\nDavid M.
